    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer864667673142668072">Nobel Prize-winner Jose Saramago's brilliant new novel poses the question what happens when the grim reaper decides there will be no more death? On the first day of the new year, no one dies. This, of course, causes consternation among politicians, religious leaders, morticians, and doctors. Among the general public, on the other hand, there is initially mass celebration. </span>
  <span id="freeText864667673142668072" style="display:none">Nobel Prize-winner Jose Saramago's brilliant new novel poses the question what happens when the grim reaper decides there will be no more death? On the first day of the new year, no one dies. This, of course, causes consternation among politicians, religious leaders, morticians, and doctors. Among the general public, on the other hand, there is initially mass celebration. Flags are hung out on balconies; people dance in the streets. They have achieved the great goal of humanity: eternal life. Then reality hits home‚Äîfamilies are left to care for the permanently dying; life-insurance policies become meaningless; and funeral parlors are reduced to arranging burials for pet dogs, cats, hamsters, and parrots. <br /><br />Death sits in her chilly apartment, where she lives alone with scythe and filing cabinets and contemplates her experiment: What if no one ever died again? What if she, death with a small "d," became human and were to fall in love?</span>
  <a data-text-id="864667673142668072" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9336534853532434873">What happens if people stop dying? After a general celebration many people are actually in trouble: undertakers, those who work for cemeteries and insurance companies and others. The nation passes a law that pets must be given full burials! Insurance companies change procedures so that people in their 80‚Äôs get the benefits. Even religions have a problem: if people don‚Äôt expect to die‚Ä¶.?<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570225048i/28245442._SX540_.jpg' width='440' height='330' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Lady Death has taken a holiday in this one country. (And she is a she in languages with gendered pronouns.) But</span>
  <span id="freeText9336534853532434873" style="display:none">What happens if people stop dying? After a general celebration many people are actually in trouble: undertakers, those who work for cemeteries and insurance companies and others. The nation passes a law that pets must be given full burials! Insurance companies change procedures so that people in their 80‚Äôs get the benefits. Even religions have a problem: if people don‚Äôt expect to die‚Ä¶.?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570225048i/28245442._SX540_.jpg" width="440" height="330" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Lady Death has taken a holiday in this one country. (And she is a she in languages with gendered pronouns.) But people still age. Now Grandma will be suffering up in that corner bedroom indefinitely. Some people start smuggling the very elderly across the border where they die and are buried. A new industry grows up around that. Are they heroes or villains? A lot of the early part of the book is a cynical description of political compromise and government inefficiency and corruption. <br><br>After all this ruckus, Death (although she prefers using the lower case ‚Äòd‚Äô) decides to send a letter to the prime minister of the country announcing that Death will resume her duties but that in the future everyone will receive a purple envelope with a letter announcing their death a week in advance so they can put their affairs in order. <br><br>But one letter keeps getting returned by the post office ‚Äì nothing mystical, probably just the usual bureaucratic screw-ups. Death leaves her cold, windowless office and decides to investigate. The person the letter is addressed to is a middle-aged man, a cellist, living a solitary life with his dog. Death starts spying on him as home without being seen, but then assumes human form and starts going to his concerts‚Ä¶<br><br>Some lines I liked:<br><br>‚Äú‚Ä¶but neither did we want merely to relegate it to the darkness of the inkwell.‚Äù<br><br>‚Äú‚Ä¶they went out for wool and came home shorn.‚Äù<br><br>‚ÄúOne cannot be too careful with words, they change their minds just as people do.‚Äù <br><br>The book has chapter breaks but is structured in page-length paragraphs with mostly commas. There is occasional dialogue but no quotation marks. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570225048i/28245443._SX540_.jpg" width="440" height="330" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A good read although not my favorite by Nobel Prize winner Saramago. <br></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1622549388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17871973834637181974"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1285555.Jos__Saramago' title='Jos√© Saramago' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jos√© Saramago</a>'s imagination appeals to me: I cherished <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3018539.Death_With_Interruptions' title='Death With Interruptions by Jos√© Saramago' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death with Interruptions</a> from the first page. It's was not only the author‚Äôs incredible creativity or his masterful writing but also the fact that here he deals with something so close to all human beings: <i>
  <b>death.</b>
</i> Who, after all, at some point in life, hasn't asked <i>why do we have to die?</i> The dream of immortality has fascinated humanity forever.<br/><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>"The following day, no one died."</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>So simply<i>Saramago</i> begins: in an undisclosed small E</span>
  <span id="freeText17871973834637181974" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1285555.Jos__Saramago" title="Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Jos√© Saramago</a>'s imagination appeals to me: I cherished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3018539.Death_With_Interruptions" title="Death With Interruptions by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Death with Interruptions</a> from the first page. It's was not only the author‚Äôs incredible creativity or his masterful writing but also the fact that here he deals with something so close to all human beings: <i>
  <b>death.</b>
</i> Who, after all, at some point in life, hasn't asked <i>why do we have to die?</i> The dream of immortality has fascinated humanity forever.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>"The following day, no one died."</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>So simply<i>Saramago</i> begins: in an undisclosed small European country, without any logic or harbinger, people suddenly stopped dying.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>"Having lived, until those days of confusion, in what they had imagined to be the best of all possible and probable worlds, they were discovering, with delight, that the best, the absolute best, was happening right now, right there, at the door of their house, a unique and marvelous life without the daily fear of parca‚Äôs creaking scissors, immortality in the land that gave us our being, safe from any metaphysical awkwardnesses and free to everyone, with no sealed orders to open at the hour of our death, announcing at that crossroads where dear companions in this vale of tears known as earth were forced to part and set off for their different destinations in the next world, you to paradise, you to purgatory, you down to hell."</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>A dream come through, correct?<br /><br />But nothing is for free on this earth of ours, and very soon the exultation starts to die down. As the days goes by what we discover in Saramago‚Äôs fascinating story is ultimately thousands of people suspended on the edge of dying. Slowly the country finds itself disoriented for what to do, immersed in a new confusion. People lose jobs (no more jobs for undertakers or gravediggers, and so many others that depended on it). Religion has lost its reason and its greatest reward, resurrection. And philosophers are left speechless facing the fact of a <i>
  <b>‚Äúsociety torn between the hope of living forever and the fear of never dying.‚Äù</b>
</i><br /><br />How everything seems to have turned upside down: life is now the inconvenience: <i>
  <b>"If we don‚Äôt start dying again, we have no future."</b>
</i> Apart from any judgment on Saramago's religious feelings that probably dwell underneath his novel or any religion discussion, his unique narration is so superbly delivered that it conquers the reader. And he is always amazing us: <br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>"Due to some strange optical phenomenon, real or virtual, death seems much smaller now as if her bones had shrunk, or perhaps she was always like that, and it's our eyes, wide with fear, that make her look like a giant. Poor death. It makes us feel like going over and putting a hand on her hard shoulder and whispering a few words of sympathy in her ear, or, rather, in the place where her ear once was, underneath the parietal."</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><i>Saramago</i> simply captivates the reader. Read it if you enjoy something that will leave you with much more than with what you started before opening one of his books. As his literature Nobel Prize attests, he knows how to write. He is brilliant, and you won‚Äôt forget him after the first taste.<br />____</span>

<div id="review_237146211" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237146211" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237146211?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2011-reads">2011-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads">excellent-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237146211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14704905243618524019"><br/>The dream of immortality has always fascinated humanity. The dream of eternal life has founded religions that changed the shape of the world. What if it were true?<blockquote>"<b><i>The following day, no one died.</i></b>"</blockquote>So begins Jos√© Saramago's <i>Death with Interruptions</i>. In an unnamed small European country without any explanations people have stopped dying - <b>an eternal dream come true, right?</b><br/><br/>What else can we want now, once the threat of unavoidable demise has been removed seemingly forever, once the unstoppable Grim R</span>
  <span id="freeText14704905243618524019" style="display:none"><br>The dream of immortality has always fascinated humanity. The dream of eternal life has founded religions that changed the shape of the world. What if it were true?<blockquote>"<b><i>The following day, no one died.</i></b>"</blockquote>So begins Jos√© Saramago's <i>Death with Interruptions</i>. In an unnamed small European country without any explanations people have stopped dying - <b>an eternal dream come true, right?</b><br><br>What else can we want now, once the threat of unavoidable demise has been removed seemingly forever, once the unstoppable Grim Reaper seems to have retired?<blockquote>"<i>Having lived, until those days of confusion, in what they had imagined to be the best of all possible and probable worlds, they were discovering, with delight, that the best, the absolute best, was happening right now, right there, at the door of their house, a unique and marvelous life without the daily fear of parca‚Äôs creaking scissors, immortality in the land that gave us our being, safe from any metaphysical awkwardnesses and free to everyone, with no sealed orders to open at the hour of our death, announcing at that crossroads where dear companions in this vale of tears known as earth were forced to part and set off for their different destinations in the next world, you to paradise, you to purgatory, you down to hell.</i>"<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363409i/714733.jpg" width="330" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><b>Well, once the celebrations died down, it quickly becomes obvious that this paradise on earth comes at a price.</b> Immortality is not eternal youth, and ultimately what we have is hundreds and thousands of people suspended on the edge of dying, in the in-between state, neither dead nor alive, caught on the borderline. <blockquote>"<i>...One must admit that the prospects are not just gloomy, they're terrible, catastrophic, more dangerous by far than anything even the wildest imagination could dream up.</i>"</blockquote>And slowly it sinks in that before long it's not only the undertakers and gravediggers who are out of jobs; not only religion that becomes obsolete as its greatest reward - resurrection - is no longer a big deal <i>(seriously, Saramago's distaste for religion is very prominently underscored and explained in this book)</i>; not only the philosophical schools left pointless and speechless; it's generations and generations in <i>"this society torn between the hope of living forever and the fear of never dying"</i> who will have to dedicate themselves to caring for the millions of not-quite-dead; it's the country unprepared to care for the citizens who are no longer free to enjoy the certainty of death. <br><br><b>Life itself has quickly become the burden - oh how the tables have turned!</b> <blockquote>"<i>If we don‚Äôt start dying again, we have no future.</i>"</blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363409i/714734.jpg" width="215" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363409i/714735.jpg" width="180" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And all of this is delivered in Saramago's unique rambling narration, with margin-to-margin solid text uninterrupted even by dialogue marks, jumping from topic to topic, zeroing on details and mundane, running in the clustered run-on sentences. It's all delivered in the voice that is both dry and witty, detached yet flourishing, both mocking and serious. It's not an easy style to read, especially in this book, with meandering narration only underscoring the absence of easily definable plot, the absence of characters who we can follow and love and root for. <br><br>It's like an introduction, a leisurely essay stretching for 64% of the book - the story that somehow, despite (or maybe because of!) all this kept the magical charm over me, grounding the most improbable of the storyline in the firm reality anchored by human folly and bureaucracy and greed and crime and incredulity.<br><br>And then, almost two-thirds into the story, the mood shifts, the narration abruptly changes, and the new plot emerges, folding violet letters into violet envelopes, confidently raising its head and wondering, <i>Have you missed me? I came to steal your heart.</i> <b>And the strangest love story begins, having nudged the meandering weary satirical narration out of the way.</b><br><br>It's <b>death</b>, the female noun in so many languages, whose whim led to such perturbances in the function of the state and religion and philosophy. It's death, who is surprised at the audacity of a mediocre unremarkable middle-aged musician who refuses to die. It's death, a stranger to failure, who sets out to investigate and to set the matters right, unprepared for what is waiting for her.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363409i/714738.jpg" width="220" class="gr-hostedUserImg">*<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363409i/714739.jpg" width="171" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><blockquote>"<i>Due to some strange optical phenomenon, real or virtual, death seems much smaller now, as if her bones had shrunk, or perhaps she was always like that, and it's our eyes, wide with fear, that make her look like a giant. Poor death. It makes us feel like going over and putting a hand on her hard shoulder and whispering a few words of sympathy in her ear, or, rather, in the place where her ear once was, underneath the parietal.</i>"</blockquote><b>It's where Saramago's prose, his entire narration undergoes a fascinating transformation.</b> No, his style does not change. We still have solid blocks of text and meandering ramblings and endless strangely punctuated sentences, but the slow shift in the mood and the feeling subtly creeps up making you look up from the book and wonder - am I still reading the same story? And why do I have those pesky tears glistening in the corners of my eyes? And why can't I stop myself from sighing and quietly saying, <i>"Aww...."</i> at the end?<br><br>You know why? Because the guy who wrote this book has received a Nobel Prize in literature for a reason. Because he is brilliant, that's why. And because he decided to play around with this story, leaving us - or at least me - unable to resist its pull. <b>And so I stand by my 5-star rating from a year ago</b>, and begin a desperate hunt for more Saramago books.<blockquote>"<i>For the first time in her life, death knew what it felt like to have a dog on her lap.</i>"</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14704905243618524019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_214496531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/214496531" />
    <a title="s.penkevich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich"><img alt="s.penkevich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587089836p2/6431467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/214496531?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="s.penkevich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="s.penkevich" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich">s.penkevich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone with an imagination</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=saramago">saramago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer214496531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15180620706698958759">Out of the half dozen Saramago novels I have read, this is actually my favorite. It may have been due in part that I devoured most of it while seated upon the sun soaked banks of a river this past July, but this short little work really struck me. It is so unique and imaginative and this book was just a really fun read. Despite it's focus of death and all, it isn't quite as heavy as most of his novels and will make you laugh at the dark abyss of death as most of this novel is actually darkly hum</span>
  <span id="freeText15180620706698958759" style="display:none">Out of the half dozen Saramago novels I have read, this is actually my favorite. It may have been due in part that I devoured most of it while seated upon the sun soaked banks of a river this past July, but this short little work really struck me. It is so unique and imaginative and this book was just a really fun read. Despite it's focus of death and all, it isn't quite as heavy as most of his novels and will make you laugh at the dark abyss of death as most of this novel is actually darkly humorous. There is no traditional plot for the first two thirds of the novel as Saramago displays his story with a broad shot that encompasses all facets of his deathless phenomenon. <br /><br />The first part of the novel is more or less Saramago's imagination exploring all sides of his idea. Saramago takes something most people would view as a great joy - to live forever - and puts it on an ugly display as a terrifying curse. Namely, just because you live forever doesn't mean you don't suffer bodily harm. He tells of people with their guts spilled out somehow living on and other horrific conditions to a similar effect. He goes on to explain how this also practically ruins the economy and brings about the maphia (who choose this with a 'ph' to separate themselves from the regular mafia) who create more undying corpses if you don't bow to their wishes. Wow. What a disaster of a world is made in the first 100 pages.<br /><br />In the second section of the novel, Saramago zooms in and shows this event on a small scale; his major focus is on death herself and how she relates to the world. Saramago's death character was fascinating and different than any traditional image of death (speaking of tradition death, there is a funny bit where the government takes all the traditional images of death and uses technology to see what these skull images would look like with a human face) and he actually manages to make death a likeable, empathizable character. I won't go into the plot and spoil what happens in case you have not yet read this, but I never thought I'd read a book about Death as a main character and describe it as 'cute' and like it for that. Saramago once again does the impossible and all I can say is that after the last page you can't help but say "aww". <br /><br />As a note of caution, Saramago has a unique style that tends to turn people away and this slightly bothers me. It is NOT difficult to read, give it a few pages and I promise you will grasp it. It flows surprisingly well. Also, Saramago has a very distinct voice that I can't get enough of. He speaks directly to you as a reader and he talks at his characters in a very fatherly, loving fashion that lets you see how proud he is of his own creations. He has a very good way of telling a story, often justifying his reasons for why he chose to tell it the way he does in a funny, unique manner. I would highly recommend this to any Saramago fan, and to anyone new to this Nobel laureate's works although I think Blindness might be a better starting point.<br /><b>
  <i>4/5</i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15180620706698958759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1821052470?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1821052470" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14789302255397539550">I love the cover of this book, the cartoon woman in black, paused on the doorstep of someone's life, her symbolic scythe held aloft. A light switch features in the centre of the illustration as if she might jokingly dim the lights while she fulfills her task. We almost expect to see a grin on her face and the illustrator has kindly left her features blank so that we can fill in that smirk for ourselves. A perfect book cover for a satire about death. <br/>I‚Äôm not so keen on the title however; Death At</span>
  <span id="freeText14789302255397539550" style="display:none">I love the cover of this book, the cartoon woman in black, paused on the doorstep of someone's life, her symbolic scythe held aloft. A light switch features in the centre of the illustration as if she might jokingly dim the lights while she fulfills her task. We almost expect to see a grin on her face and the illustrator has kindly left her features blank so that we can fill in that smirk for ourselves. A perfect book cover for a satire about death. <br />I‚Äôm not so keen on the title however; Death At Intervals sounds odd to me. The original title was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/327335.As_Intermit_ncias_da_Morte" title="As Intermit√™ncias da Morte by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">As Intermit√™ncias da Morte</a>, and when the book was translated into English, there were two versions of the title, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1456607.Death_at_Intervals" title="Death at Intervals by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Death at Intervals</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3018539.Death_with_Interruptions" title="Death with Interruptions by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Death with Interruptions</a> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the French for 'light switch' is 'interrupteur' <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I prefer the second version but I‚Äôd have really liked a version that stuck closer to the Portuguese and used the lovely word ‚Äòintermittences‚Äô, a word which always make me think of those heart-stopping moments in life when we suddenly recall things that had previously been buried too deep to remember. I‚Äôve taken that idea from Proust, of course, who had many 'intermittences of the heart' moments so it‚Äôs interesting to note that Saramago mentions Proust in relation to the 'final' heart-stopping moment of life. Yes, Saramago‚Äôs narrator refers not once but three times to the woman in black whom Proust reported seeing in a corner of his room just before he died, so I imagine Proust‚Äôs dark figure must have been some sort of trigger for this humorous tale about a woman called death, even if it may not be the sole inspiration. In fact Saramago wrote this book in 2005 at a time when his own health was poor; he was eighty-three and suffering from leukaemia and there was a point around then when his own death was interrupted: he was pronounced dead but recovered and lived on until 2010. Two years before he died, he wrote in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7114856.The_Notebook" title="The Notebook by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">The Notebook</a>, <i>The truth is, I feel myself alive, very much alive, whenever for one reason or other I have to talk about death.</i> As we read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3018539.Death_with_Interruptions" title="Death with Interruptions by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Death with Interruptions</a> we really appreciate the truth of that statement. It‚Äôs as if Saramago managed to access some memory about death that the rest of us have repressed. This allows him to treat death in an audaciously irreverent way, and we are fairly certain that he has a wide grin on his face as he spins out the narrative, aiming delightful darts at his favourite targets in the process; the Church is not spared, nor are politicians; royalty also comes in for some satire, as do many of society‚Äôs institutions. It is all so smoothly done that we can only stand back and admire the perfect blend of what is said with the way it is said. <br />No one writes quite like Saramago.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="14789302255397539550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText15376014399039930715" style="display:none"><b>Lethal But Not Morbid</b><br /><br />A great theological/philosophical book of ideas about how human beings deal with death - as a concept as well as their own individual fate. Saramago knows what most of us know but don't know how to say. He knows how politicians and academics and policemen and peasants talk and what they mean when they talk, which is often the opposite of what they say. And his gentle irony accepts the fact that we all lie by inevitable omission every time we utter a sound. So death for example may be lethal but perhaps it is not morbid. Perhaps it too can be embraced in life.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1928517627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1928517627" />
    <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan"><img alt="Jr Bacdayan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552066595p2/16587936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1928517627?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jr Bacdayan" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1928517627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17273485932046051841">Jose Saramago‚Äôs Death with Interruptions is a fascinating study of death and its implications to humanity. The book can be separated into two parts, the first part is a study of conceptual death or more accurately the loss thereof, and how it would affect the lives of the mortal beings suddenly deemed immortal. Then about two-thirds into the book, death suddenly takes another entity, from a formerly conceptual standpoint we are gradually introduced to death personified. Death is a woman, a beaut</span>
  <span id="freeText17273485932046051841" style="display:none">Jose Saramago‚Äôs Death with Interruptions is a fascinating study of death and its implications to humanity. The book can be separated into two parts, the first part is a study of conceptual death or more accurately the loss thereof, and how it would affect the lives of the mortal beings suddenly deemed immortal. Then about two-thirds into the book, death suddenly takes another entity, from a formerly conceptual standpoint we are gradually introduced to death personified. Death is a woman, a beautiful woman, who takes interest upon a lone mortal who evades her scythe.<br /><br />Initially I found this rather tedious. With a macro narrative that detailed events of a nameless monarchial country, more interested in industries than people, it offered an interesting enough chronicle anchored on the premise that death evaded all the inhabitants of a single country at the impetus of a new year. With only a mild interest to sustain me, I ambled through the pages half-heartedly. But as it progressed death slowly gained, like it slurped away life from a gravely ailing elderly, qualities of a human being. The last third of the novel flashed by me. Death, I learned, is far more fascinating as a person than a concept. The part is dedicated to the portrayal of man‚Äôs only valid reason to want the end of death: love of another.<br /><br />As a rather imaginative story dealing with a very familiar yet unwelcome theme, it manages to paint death in a fair and, ironically, impersonal light without being emotional or needlessly sentimental. It is even somewhat comical as it shows the ridiculousness of our species when we fear it one moment, but potentially long for it the moment it no longer plagues us. This book is filled with wisdom about death, life, our tendencies as mortal creatures. There are snippets, conversations, which surprised me with sly intelligence and wry wit. To some degree maybe the parts, the <i>consonantia</i>, as Aquinas would have put it, are better than the <i>integritas</i>, or the whole. Yes, the entirety of the novel does seem a bit fragmented, however its ability to penetrate an issue such as death and its spirited treatment of it deserves high praise. <br /><br />Death is a part of life; death is a quality that makes us human, death is even an economic pillar. When taken away, as in this book, it poses doubts about life, questions humanity, trivializes love, destabilizes our economy, and mocks our fear of an integral aspect of our nature.<br /><br />What is there to fear then? Perhaps it will leave an aftertaste?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17273485932046051841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1033081738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1033081738" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1033081738?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1033081738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6110043350647504459"><a href='https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png' width='350' height='117' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/><blockquote>The following day, no one died.</blockquote><br/><br/>Oh, humanity, always wishing for what we cannot have. Eternal life, the eternal dream. But what if it came true? What if no one died? <b>Well, this is what happens in this book.</b><br/><br/>The day is normal, nothing seems out of place‚Ä¶ except people are not dying anymore. Dream come true, right? Well, you always have to be careful what you wish for. When people say they want to live forever, they think youth and health come as granted, but death‚Äôs plans were only to give hers</span>
  <span id="freeText6110043350647504459" style="display:none"><a href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" width="350" height="117" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><blockquote>The following day, no one died.</blockquote><br><br>Oh, humanity, always wishing for what we cannot have. Eternal life, the eternal dream. But what if it came true? What if no one died? <b>Well, this is what happens in this book.</b><br><br>The day is normal, nothing seems out of place‚Ä¶ except people are not dying anymore. Dream come true, right? Well, you always have to be careful what you wish for. When people say they want to live forever, they think youth and health come as granted, but death‚Äôs plans were only to give herself a vacation from killing people, not to stop time and heal everything.<br><br>So starts a conflict in which people start to freak out and realise the problem that is no more deaths. As it happened in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness__Blindness___1_" title="Blindness (Blindness, #1) by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Blindness</a>, the realism of it all is striking. When people get desperate, stupid decisions come with it as well, and things get even more complicated for them because they don‚Äôt know what to do with all the bodies of people who were supposed to be corpses.<br><br>But death has more plans. She decides to play even more with people, and she wants to have more fun. In the process, however, she starts to feel love. Love towards the life humans live, and‚Ä¶ is she falling in love with someone? What would that represent? How hard can life get if she, the only with the power to kill when it pleases her, decides she likes humans more than her original form?<br><br>There are many important themes addressed in this book. For example, religion is one of them. Saramago‚Äôs opinion is not surprising considering he was an atheist. You can guess what some of his messages were. He never meant to insult religious people, so don‚Äôt worry about that.<br><br>Character wise‚Ä¶ there‚Äôs not much development, and it‚Äôs not really necessary because the book is more composed of ‚Äúwhat if‚Äùs and social consequences of an initial action. However, I must say, death (yes, without capital d) is a fantastic character. She‚Äôs so‚Ä¶ human. Unexpectedly human, one would think. We imagine that skeleton with a scythe, but she‚Äôs a woman, with feelings, and all, and a scythe with whom she talks too.<br><br>The writing gets a little to get used to. If you‚Äôve read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness__Blindness___1_" title="Blindness (Blindness, #1) by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Blindness</a> or any other book by Saramago, then you already know he does not use quotation marks to denote dialogue, so you have to guess when and who is talking. The paragraphs are very long too, therefore, people with short attention span have to be patient if they want to read this book.<br><br>Please don‚Äôt be scared about this latter point, though. I promise you the book is worth it, and the concept is very interesting, and keeps interesting throughout the book. Plus, Saramago was an amazing writer, with a well-deserved Nobel Prize and all. Now I want to continue his works with reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47667.Seeing__Blindness___2_" title="Seeing (Blindness, #2) by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Seeing</a>, which is the sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness__Blindness___1_" title="Blindness (Blindness, #1) by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Blindness</a>, another fantastic book I also recommend wholeheartedly.<br><br>Answer me this question now: Do you want to know what the real consequences of an hypothetical eternal life are? Do you want to live forever? If the you say yes to the first one, then read this.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10969745135334900875">I can't believe I'm writing this, because I NEVER like magical realism, but this book was brilliant! So strange, yes, but so thought provoking and somehow very tenderly written, too. I definitely want to read more by this author and am really glad I stumbled upon this odd sounding book and gave it a chance. Margaret Jull Costa, the translator, did a wonderful job, because the language and writing style was, in part, what made this book stand out so much for me. Death with Interruptions is defini</span>
  <span id="freeText10969745135334900875" style="display:none">I can't believe I'm writing this, because I NEVER like magical realism, but this book was brilliant! So strange, yes, but so thought provoking and somehow very tenderly written, too. I definitely want to read more by this author and am really glad I stumbled upon this odd sounding book and gave it a chance. Margaret Jull Costa, the translator, did a wonderful job, because the language and writing style was, in part, what made this book stand out so much for me. Death with Interruptions is definitely not going to be for everyone, but maybe it's because I read it at a time where so many are dying, so much tragedy is all around us, that it seemed strange and intriguing to contemplate death with the human face Saramago lends it. I'll be thinking about this one for some time to come.<br /><br />Find my book reviews and more at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.princessandpen.com">http://www.princessandpen.com</a></span>

<div id="review_2008930157" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2008930157" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2008930157?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846550201" href="/book/show/1456607.Death_at_Intervals">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2008930157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17777183075607694310">Saramago‚Äôs novels often have the appearance of grand allegories, but they are not. One gets the sense of a writer simply exploring a premise with great freedom and a lack of embarrassment. Saramago allows himself to follow a line of thought to its logical conclusion, even if that conclusion is absurd to the point of ridiculousness. What makes this work is that he does not expect you to suspend disbelief. This is an exercise in conjecture, not realism. Where there are nonsensical contradictions, </span>
  <span id="freeText17777183075607694310" style="display:none">Saramago‚Äôs novels often have the appearance of grand allegories, but they are not. One gets the sense of a writer simply exploring a premise with great freedom and a lack of embarrassment. Saramago allows himself to follow a line of thought to its logical conclusion, even if that conclusion is absurd to the point of ridiculousness. What makes this work is that he does not expect you to suspend disbelief. This is an exercise in conjecture, not realism. Where there are nonsensical contradictions, he is the first to point them out and laugh along with you. <br /><br />Saramago begins <i>Death at Intervals</i> with very broad strokes. He is poking around, trying to kick up a worthy subject from under the dust, and it is clear from this haphazard approach that he really has no idea of the direction the novel will take. The scope narrows suddenly and dramatically in the second half of the novel, as he latches onto the single, salient figure that this rummaging has found for him. The entire thing is as absurd as it is refreshing. The surprising strangeness of the second half of the novel justifies the meanderings of the first. <br /><br />But with Saramagos‚Äôs writing, it‚Äôs very much about the journey, not the destination. The author leaves no stone unturned, no question unasked, as he takes us on this strange trip. The characters themselves guide his writing, and they are allowed to roam unbounded within his imagination. There are so many surprising turns, so many dark corners of human experience that are uncovered in this free exploration, that are not acknowledged by any other writer. <i>Death at Intervals</i> is strange and flawed, but Saramago‚Äôs approach is just so unusual, and his imagination so free ‚Äì it really is a wonderfully unique experience.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17777183075607694310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3403161018?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Th√©odore " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Th√©odore " href="/user/show/98232817-th-odore">Th√©odore </a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3403161018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2574287587486113955">Have you ever wondered what it would be like to live forever ?<br/> But if eternal life would not coincide with the cessation of bodily degradation that old age entails, the dissapearance of death could suddenly no longer be such an attractive idea.<br/><br/>"The next day, no one died ".<br/><br/>This is how Saramago begins his novel. Obviously, the first reaction is joy, with people feeling like they have just escaped the biggest fear of their lives, and that from that moment on, their whole existence will change radic</span>
  <span id="freeText2574287587486113955" style="display:none">Have you ever wondered what it would be like to live forever ?<br /> But if eternal life would not coincide with the cessation of bodily degradation that old age entails, the dissapearance of death could suddenly no longer be such an attractive idea.<br /><br />"The next day, no one died ".<br /><br />This is how Saramago begins his novel. Obviously, the first reaction is joy, with people feeling like they have just escaped the biggest fear of their lives, and that from that moment on, their whole existence will change radically. <br /> But people continue though, to have all the suffering that disease and old age bring. At this point, people come to realize that the fear felt throughout their life, towards death - it is less intense than that generated by the absence of death. Saramago launches, in this context, in the analysis of human feelings towards the fatality of death. These are put face to face, with peoples position on divinity, the idea being that death has a much more active and powerful presence in people's lives than God. (the spelling folows the one proposed by the narrator).<br /><br />This quasi-bogumilic thesis aims to reconsider the most intense desires. Ultimately, aversion to death - is just a fear of the unknown.<br /><br />The moment Death becomes a character, and interacts with people directly - the way we relate to it - changes.<br /> Death becomes an active and visible character when he decides to warn those who are going to die, that they have only one week to live. <br />The only problem occurs when one of the letters of this type, sent by Death - does not reach the adresant. Here, Death decides to mingle with people, to see why his letter did not arrive. <br />Thus, we find an interesting character, a violinist - who feels that he has missed his whole life. <br />Death seems unable to penetrate the intimacy of man who should have another chance at gratitude, to salvation from regret for eternity. <br />This story being the most beautiful of the novel, I let you discover its finality by yourself. <br /><br />The initial interpretation can be reconsidered when , at the last sentence, I discover that this is the same sentence with which Saramago begins his story.<br />So, are we dealing with a cyclical novel ? I think not, which means that the key to unraveling the mystery of the death- defying violinist lies precisely in this suggestion of cyclicity. <br /><br />In addition to the creativity and novelty of the theme, the novel is worth reading, because it is one of the most beautiful demonstration of how suspence is constructed in a narrative, without becoming commercial, in the bad sense of the word.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2574287587486113955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2741100328" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer346088019876167122">Yes, This is It! This short-sized and small length book (~ 250 pages) made me knock-out, or, paraphrasing, the small stone overthrows the big cart. It successfully targeted my 'Achilles' heel' (well, each one of us is a potential Achilles). So, in other words, it made me fail the bet I made with myself that I won't read anything else but only Saramago until I'll devour and satisfy myself with all his existing printed out books. That was my Olympic marathon. It was a thing I was proud of, especia</span>
  <span id="freeText346088019876167122" style="display:none">Yes, This is It! This short-sized and small length book (~ 250 pages) made me knock-out, or, paraphrasing, the small stone overthrows the big cart. It successfully targeted my 'Achilles' heel' (well, each one of us is a potential Achilles). So, in other words, it made me fail the bet I made with myself that I won't read anything else but only Saramago until I'll devour and satisfy myself with all his existing printed out books. That was my Olympic marathon. It was a thing I was proud of, especially that I'm (raising) head over heels in a clashing and crushing amour with this Portuguese writer. I guess that because of too much spellbound charm I got myself 'blinded' (could be truth in saying that love is blind) and even extra deep sensitive. The novel Death with Interruptions had to be the one to make me realize that indeed I am not a super-hero-god to disregard anything with the same attitude of 'everything is possible, nothing is impossible'.<br />It is a story about death (with small ‚Äòd‚Äô, and yes, a correct guess, death is a 'she', wonder why!) having decided to go on strike, for a period of approx. 7-8 months, without any pre-warning or secondary details shared with the population of an unnamed country, still somewhere in recent times as all mass media is strongly present in the scenery. Not surprisingly, a complete economic and social breakdown ensues in the unnamed country (though a monarchy..). The implications on all the social and economic stakeholders are terrifying, shocking, dizzying, eventually apocalyptic. The book itself abounds with a completely challenging theme. But, as always and ever again, it is the playfulness, the language, the wit and irony that glued me to this book. An original, critical and utterly entertaining fantasy.<br />Saramago's narrator is in no hurry to tell the reason(s) why the ‚Äòdeath‚Äô took this unexpected step. He just starts outlining all the consequences of this unprecedented event and what big calamity it turns out to be for entire mankind, although it seems for animals and vegetal worlds another death, maybe her sister or cousin, is still proceeding with work as usual, ending lives when the clock strikes the right time (could there ever be a right time to depart!?, so it seems) or the hour glass has finished all the sand counting‚Ä¶ There is a high-quality humor on every page, the narrator speaking directly to the reader and, time and again, even apologizing for "offending against the rules of storytelling." How is that? Just makes me solidify my every blooming smiles and grins...<br />Everything he writes about leads to insightful comments that deepens the novel. He is an amazing novelist, letting his engaging and entertaining stories speak for themselves. What can be best (not just better) but to tell tales about things he has either seen or invented, experienced or imagined, as if to possess the supreme art of being able to blur the frontiers between the two.<br />I feel myself convinced that when a highly appreciated book finds you at the right moment in life, that becomes integrated and intrinsically stitched into your overall identity. This book should be the soulless one of Saramago (I mean ‚Äòdeath‚Äô is so), but no, it‚Äôs just the opposite. It has a soul, a big one, which the genius of Saramago proposes and delivers to his readers with all the amazing mix of his stunning writing of witty, intelligent, loving, crazy, magical, abounding generosity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="346088019876167122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_35177812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35177812" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1289391-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1215616814p2/1289391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35177812?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1289391-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35177812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17233286152370887524">Saramago's wonderful novel takes the old motif of death taking a holiday and breathes new life into it. Stylistically challenging for the reader with its run-on sentences and eschewing of capitals other than those that are initial, the work demands concentration. For those willing to put some effort into it, however, it becomes an experience very much like thinking the author's thoughts with him. As he leads us through the narrative, Saramago takes time to criticize government, business, religio</span>
  <span id="freeText17233286152370887524" style="display:none">Saramago's wonderful novel takes the old motif of death taking a holiday and breathes new life into it. Stylistically challenging for the reader with its run-on sentences and eschewing of capitals other than those that are initial, the work demands concentration. For those willing to put some effort into it, however, it becomes an experience very much like thinking the author's thoughts with him. As he leads us through the narrative, Saramago takes time to criticize government, business, religion, and organized crime. As a whole, the work examines the question of what it means to be human when all of us stand under the hanging wave of death's tsunami and will in the end be swept to oblivion. There are insights to be found throughout the text, not the least of which is a compassionate and empathetic understanding of the relationship between a man and his dog. While purporting to give us death's views on the never-ending task of putting an end to life, the novel engages us in a consideration of the things that make life worth living, even in the shadow of death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17233286152370887524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11102247529620868691">Attention please! <br/>Cease your work and grab this book.<br/>Phenomenal piece of writing.<br/><br/>The most striking line ever to start a novel. <br/><b>THE FOLLOWING DAY, NO ONE DIED.</b><br/><br/>One of the finest book I have ever read.<br/>I bet no one has written anything a bit similar to this.<br/>If you haven't read it yet, you're missing something remarkable.<br/>Hands down to this absolutely fascinating, terribly terrific, painstakingly beautiful, exceptional masterpiece.   <span id="freeText11102247529620868691" style="display:none">Attention please! <br />Cease your work and grab this book.<br />Phenomenal piece of writing.<br /><br />The most striking line ever to start a novel. <br /><b>THE FOLLOWING DAY, NO ONE DIED.</b><br /><br />One of the finest book I have ever read.<br />I bet no one has written anything a bit similar to this.<br />If you haven't read it yet, you're missing something remarkable.<br />Hands down to this absolutely fascinating, terribly terrific, painstakingly beautiful, exceptional masterpiece. For me definitely one of the all-time great works. Sir Jos√© Saramago is one of my top favorite writers. He's incredibly genius having <br />Top-notch writing style <br />Punctilious philosophy<br />Miraculous plot,<br />Exceptional circumstances <br />&amp; what not. <br />Precisely this man is unrivalled.<br /><br />Whatever he wrote is improbable to think, believe or imagine. But he has written all in books with so perfection that you are forced to believe &amp; go with the flow.<br />It's impossible to compile all my thoughts in one review. My words can't do justice with his sheer brilliance but I can't let it go without saying anything.<br /><br />How can one write a review when it's all about death and it's ruthlessness.<br />And when the starting line of the book is:<br /><i>THE FOLLOWING DAY, NO ONE DIED.</i><br />I mean how could one think <br />Death went on vacations for a particular country &amp; no one is dying. <br />Can we? NO.<br />People are about to die but death leaves &amp; then came back with all her due death tasks. <br /><br />Death speaks, <br />Wrote letters on violet coloured paper, <br />Dispatch them.<br />&amp;<br />The turning point <br />death has feelings and fall in love with the cellist.<br /><br />Strongly recommended. <br />You must must must read this one. You cannot let it go. <br />I'm telling you simply cannot.<br /><br />I was sad to see not many people has read this masterpiece yet but it's high time now. Better late than never. <br />Pick up this one &amp; read.<br /><br />The book has so much to talk about so I'm going to add all my favourite melancholic lines &amp; brilliant excerpts here:<br /><br /><i>There would probably have been no salvation for this particular patient, but one thing was clear, today, the victim refused to die.<i></i><br /><br /><br /><i>What the hell is going on with the government, who have so far given not the slightest sign of life.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>A philosopher from the optimistic wing, why are you so alarmed by the fact that death has ended, We don't know that it has, we know only that it has ceased to kill, which is not the same thing. </i><br /><br /><br /><i>What would happen if we all lived forever, where would it end. </i><br /><br /><br /><i>IN THIS COUNTRY IN WHICH NO ONE DIES NOT EVERYTHING was as sordid as we have just described, nor, in this society torn between the hope of living forever and the fear of never dying. </i><br /><br /><br /><i>So the death that used to be our death has stopped working, but the others, the deaths of animals and plants, continue to operate. </i><br /><br /><br /><i>IT WAS MUCH MORE THAN A HECATOMB. THE SEVEN MONTHS that death's unilateral truce had lasted produced a waiting list of more than sixty thousand people on the point of death, or sixty-two thousand five hundred and eighty to be exact, all laid to rest in a moment, in an instant of time packed with a deadly power that would find comparison only in certain reprehensible human actions.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>By the way, we feel we must mention that death, by herself and alone, with no external help, has always killed far less than mankind has.</i><br /><br /><br />Letter by death,<br /><i>Dear madam, I regret to inform you that in a week your life will end, irrevocably and irremissibly. Please make the best use you can of the time remaining to you, yours faithfully, death. Two hundred and ninety-eight sheets of paper, two hundred and ninety-eight envelopes, two hundred and ninety-eight names removed from the list, this is not exactly a killingly hard job, but the fact is that when she reaches the end, death is exhausted. </i><br /><br /><br /><i>WE'VE ALL HAD OUR MOMENTS OF WEAKNESS, AND IF WE manage to get through today without any, we'll be sure to have some tomorrow.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>It would, in fact, be incongruous, more than that, superfluous, to find in a book that determines for each and every representative of the human race a full stop, a conclusion, an end, a death, such words as life and live, such words as I'm alive and I will live.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>These convulsive, agitated pages, emblazoned with exclamatory, apocalyptic headlines that can be folded up and put in one's pocket and carried off to be re-read at leisure in one's home and of which we are pleased to present a few of the more striking examples here, After Paradise, Hell, Death Leads The Dance, Immortal But Not For Long, Once More Condemned To Die, Checkmate, Prior Warning From Now On, No Appeal And No Hope, A Letter On Violet Paper, Sixty-Two Thousand Deaths In Less Than A Second, Death Strikes At Midnight, No Escape From Destiny, Out Of the Dream And Into the Nightmare, Return To Normal, What Did We Do To Deserve This, etcetera, etcetera.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>Death never replies, not because she doesn't want to, but because she doesn't know what to say in the face of the greatest of human sorrows.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>According to the authorized opinion of a grammarian consulted by the newspaper, death had simply failed to master even the first rudiments of the art of writing. And then, he said, there's the calligraphy, which is strangely irregular, it's as if it combined all the known ways, both possible and aberrant, of forming the letters of the latin alphabet, as if each had been written by a different person, but that could be forgiven, one could even consider it a minor defect given the chaotic syntax, the absence of full stops, the complete lack of very necessary parentheses, the obsessive elimination of paragraphs, the random use of commas and, most unforgivable sin of all, the intentional and almost diabolical abolition of the capital letter, which, can you imagine, is even omitted from the actual signature of the letter and replaced by a lower-case d. It was a disgrace, an insult, the grammarian went on, asking, If death, who has had the priceless privilege of seeing the great literary geniuses of the past, writes like this, what of our children if they choose to imitate such a philological monstrosity, on the excuse that, considering how long death has been around, she should know everything there is to know about all branches of knowledge. And the grammarian concluded, The syntactical blunders that fill this appalling letter would lead me to think that this was some huge, clumsy confidence trick were it not for grim reality and the painful evidence that the terrible threat has come to pass.</i><br /><br /><br />Reply of death to the editor:<br /><i>As we mentioned, on the afternoon of that same day, a letter from death reached the newspaper, demanding, in the most energetic terms, that the original spelling of her name be restored, Dear sir, she wrote, I am not Death, but death, Death is something of which you could never even conceive, and please note, mister grammarian, that I did not conclude that phrase with a preposition, you human beings only know the small everyday death that is me, the death which, even in the very worst disasters, is incapable of preventing life from continuing, one day you will find out about Death with a capital D, and at that moment, in the unlikely event that she gives you time to do so, you will understand the real difference between the relative and the absolute, between full and empty, between still alive and no longer alive, and when I say real difference, I am referring to something that mere words will never be able to express, relative, absolute, full, empty, still alive and no longer alive, because, sir, in case you don't know it, words move, they change from one day to the next, they are as unstable as shadows, are themselves shadows, which both are and have ceased to be, soap bubbles, shells in which one can barely hear a whisper, mere tree stumps, I give you this information gratis and for free, meanwhile, concern yourself with explaining to your readers the whys and wherefores of life and death, and now, returning to the original purpose of this letter, written, as was the one read out on television, by my own hand, I ask you to fulfill the provisions contained in the press regulations which demand that any error, omission or mistake be rectified on the same page and in the same font size, and if this letter is not published in full, sir, you run the risk of receiving tomorrow morning, with immediate effect, the prior warning that I was reserving for you in a few years' time, although, so as not to ruin the rest of your life, I won't say exactly how many, <br />yours faithfully, death. </i></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11102247529620868691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3106313047" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9848081771294071671">Death is never too far away. She follows us from the day we are born and keeps an eye on us every step of the way. She‚Äôs not waiting for the right moment to take us (back) to that dark and silent place where we were before we came to this world. She doesn‚Äôt have to wait. She needs no sense of time as she already knows exactly when that moment is going to be for each of us. She knows it all, and she‚Äôs everywhere. The time will come. <br/><br/>But what if death stopped coming to get us; If all of a sudden w</span>
  <span id="freeText9848081771294071671" style="display:none">Death is never too far away. She follows us from the day we are born and keeps an eye on us every step of the way. She‚Äôs not waiting for the right moment to take us (back) to that dark and silent place where we were before we came to this world. She doesn‚Äôt have to wait. She needs no sense of time as she already knows exactly when that moment is going to be for each of us. She knows it all, and she‚Äôs everywhere. The time will come. <br /><br />But what if death stopped coming to get us; If all of a sudden we all find out that she‚Äôs taken a holiday, or worse (better?) she‚Äôs probably not coming back? <br />What would you do?<br />What would we all do? <br />Would we all go crazy and overdose on our new found freedom?<br /><br />And so at the start of the novel we discover that no one is dying anymore. Death is nowhere to be found. Saramago has a strange but very clever way of making the reader believe the most random, disturbing and unbelievable situations without even a single drop of disbelief. There‚Äôs no ‚ÄúNah, this is bullshit‚Äù or ‚ÄúWtf, this is impossible‚Äù. No! You‚Äôre going to ‚Äúbuy‚Äù it all. You‚Äôre even going to start feeling like, after all, the implausibility it‚Äôs not that implausible. If you ever read one of his novels you‚Äôll know exactly what I mean. If you haven‚Äôt then I think it might be time for you to find out. <br /><br />But, yes, there‚Äôs a ‚Äúbut‚Äù about this one, and I‚Äôm not going to rate it five stars like I did with <i>Blindness</i> and <i>The Double</i>. Four stars for this one. Why? Well, because I feel like I‚Äôve read two different novels in just a few more than two hundred pages. Not that I think that that has to be a bad thing, but I‚Äôm only saying this because of how much quicker I was turning the pages in the second half of it. The first part lacked a main character. It was too focused on the social, political and religious effects of the absence of death, and I‚Äôm too much of a lover of strong characters to fully appreciate a story with no one in particular to love or hate.<br /><br />It wasn‚Äôt by any means bad, don‚Äôt get me wrong, that first half. Saramago‚Äôs writing was on top form (he was in his mid eighties at the time this was published) and there was plenty of food for thought but after I reached the mid-point something (really surprising and exciting) happens which meant I literally couldn‚Äôt stop turning those deadly pages! At that point there wasn‚Äôt just one but two main characters. And two very charismatic ones, by the way. Guess whose back in town!<br /><br />And the ending...! Oh my goodness, that was pure genius. <br /><br />Obrigado, Saramago.</span>

<div id="review_927892575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/927892575" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9777942-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422026319p2/9777942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/927892575?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/9777942-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9777942-joe?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer927892575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8097104560604528506">Imagine if people stopped dying in your country; what do you think would happen? What unique experiments would your nation's scientists craft to test the phenomenon? What about the dare-devils? How would the international community react? It'd be major news around the world, that's for sure. And the world's great powers would certainly want in; they'd flood your poor country with spies and outside funding. What about attempts by the terminally ill to emigrate? The potential for a humanitarian cr</span>
  <span id="freeText8097104560604528506" style="display:none">Imagine if people stopped dying in your country; what do you think would happen? What unique experiments would your nation's scientists craft to test the phenomenon? What about the dare-devils? How would the international community react? It'd be major news around the world, that's for sure. And the world's great powers would certainly want in; they'd flood your poor country with spies and outside funding. What about attempts by the terminally ill to emigrate? The potential for a humanitarian crisis, not to mention individually heart-rending stories, is almost too dramatic to think about. If nothing else, there would be an incredible public debate over what to do and what it all means.<br />	<br /><u>Death With Interruptions</u>, the tale of an unnamed country where people have stopped dieing, is not about any of this. It's about the important questions, such as what would happen to the funeral industry and what sort of black market would emerge to smuggle the comatose out of the country. It's also about Death personified as a woman who sneers at standardized punctuation and capitalization (much the way Saramago himself does) and falls in love with the man she cannot kill. Despite the plain, black cover, this book would fit best in the 'humor' section.<br />	<br />But Saramago's great blunder is not that he takes an intriguing idea and makes a joke out of it; it's that he takes his joke and bludgeons it with verbose, rambling language. As one egregious example; Saramago, after describing a border standoff over the exportation of the comatose, takes a whole page to explain why he took so long and finally settles on a single word; context. In Saramago's world, bloviation in the soul of wit.<br />	<br />This is a silly book that's too wordy to be clever and a philosophical book that's too nonsensical to take seriously. At every turn, Saramago presents us with a joke smashed by rambling anecdote, an observation drowned by irrelevance or a plot twist stifled by non sequitur. <u>Death With Interruptions</u> is not merely the worst experience I've ever had with a novel, it's the antithesis of my basic literary beliefs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8097104560604528506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
