<div id="review_1978697793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1978697793" />
    <a title="Tudor Vlad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47799008-tudor-vlad"><img alt="Tudor Vlad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494178553p2/47799008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1978697793?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tudor Vlad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tudor Vlad" href="/user/show/47799008-tudor-vlad">Tudor Vlad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47799008-tudor-vlad?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47799008-tudor-vlad?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1978697793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6079497548525951380">This is something that I did not expect myself to say, but I liked this better than <i>Blindness.</i> They are both heavy books in content, with fascinating concepts. But with <i>Death With Interruptions</i> I feel that I related more easily to the ideas that the author wanted to express. <br/><br/>We all fear death, some more than other. I for one am scared out of my wits of dying. At least when I'm in my brooding moods and start having dark thoughts. So imagine a world in which dying is out of the equation, how would</span>
  <span id="freeText6079497548525951380" style="display:none">This is something that I did not expect myself to say, but I liked this better than <i>Blindness.</i> They are both heavy books in content, with fascinating concepts. But with <i>Death With Interruptions</i> I feel that I related more easily to the ideas that the author wanted to express. <br /><br />We all fear death, some more than other. I for one am scared out of my wits of dying. At least when I'm in my brooding moods and start having dark thoughts. So imagine a world in which dying is out of the equation, how would that affect us? And what if we knew in advance when we were about to die? These are questions we all ask ourselves, but that we find difficult to answer. <br /><br />These might be difficult questions, but Jos√© Saramago does a spectacular job of trying to answer them while still leaving enough for the reader to interpret. Unlike <i>Blindness.</i> which can be classified quite easily as an extremely realistic and depressing dystopia, labeling <i>Death With Interruptions</i> is a lot more difficult, magical realism simply isn't enough. <br />This is a book that will leave you with more questions than answers and that's alright, because some questions are worth pondering on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6079497548525951380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9684822068042057900" style="display:none">Description: <i>On the first day of the new year, no one dies. This of course causes consternation among politicians, religious leaders, morticians, and doctors. Among the general public, on the other hand, there is initially celebration -‚Äî flags are hung out on balconies, people dance in the streets. They have achieved the great goal of humanity: eternal life. Then reality hits home ‚Äî- families are left to care for the permanently dying, life-insurance policies become meaningless, and funeral parlors are reduced to arranging burials for pet dogs, cats, hamsters, and parrots. Death sits in her chilly apartment, where she lives alone with scythe and filing cabinets, and contemplates her experiment: What if no one ever died again? What if she, death with a small d,became human and were to fall in love?'</i><br><br>Opening: <b>THE FOLLOWING DAY, NO ONE DIED. THIS FACT, BEING ABSOLUTELY contrary to life's rules, provoked enormous and, in circumstances, perfectly justifiable anxiety in people's minds, for we have only to consider that in the entire forty volumes of human history there is no mention, not even one exemplary case, of such a phenomenon ever having occurred, for a whole day to go by, with its generous allowance of twenty-four hours, diurnal and nocturnal, matutinal and vespertine, without one death from illness, a fatal fall, or a successful suicide, not one, not a single one.</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1347737311s/6238698.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">Oy, Saramago, you missed a chance to add crepuscular to that opening sentence; loss of impact points right there. heh.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379781093i/415353._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443445442r/16361707.gif?v=1443445449704" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>A month of Halloween 2015 reads:</b><br><br>#1: 3* Nobody True by James Herbert: fraudio<br>#2: 4* The Horror Stories of Robert E. <div id="review_1672077136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1672077136" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1672077136?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1672077136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15459863049790478171"><b>Who Needs Death?</b><br/><br/>This is the first Saramago book that I have read, but it will not be the last. I had previously been put off by the physical appearance of his text‚Äîthose rectangular blocks of gray print with no indents, no quotations, and very few paragraphs. But what I had not realized was the depth of his intelligence, the brilliance of his invention, or most importantly the extent of his wit. This is a satire, based on the simple premise that Death, or rather the small-d death responsible for</span>
  <span id="freeText15459863049790478171" style="display:none"><b>Who Needs Death?</b><br /><br />This is the first Saramago book that I have read, but it will not be the last. I had previously been put off by the physical appearance of his text‚Äîthose rectangular blocks of gray print with no indents, no quotations, and very few paragraphs. But what I had not realized was the depth of his intelligence, the brilliance of his invention, or most importantly the extent of his wit. This is a satire, based on the simple premise that Death, or rather the small-d death responsible for this particular country, takes a seven-month holiday and in all that time no one dies. A good thing? Not necessarily, because the inability to die affects also the terminally ill and the victims of horrendous road accidents, so that soon the hospitals, hospices, and eventide homes are full to bursting. The undertakers are out of work. Before long, the maphia (sic) have gone into the business of transporting moribund family members over the border so that they may end their lives elsewhere.<br /><br />But Saramago does much more than merely play with amusing what-ifs; there are philosophical implications also. There is, for instance, a telephone conversation early in the book between the prime minister and the cardinal. While the politician juggles impressively vapid press-releases, the churchman points out that without death there is no need for a church, and that it is his duty to pray for the resumption of death so that his flock may ascend to eternal life. Of all the numerous targets in the book (the author is an equal-opportunity skeptic), Saramago reserves his strongest ire for organized religion; as one of the characters remarks, "god is god and he's done almost nothing but fail." But it is not just the church that needs death; Saramago's point is that it is vital to life, and indeed we should all welcome it, even in its unpredictability.<br /><br />None of the people in the book are given proper names. Nonetheless, individual characters do begin to emerge, starting with death herself (for in romance languages the word for death is always feminine). Even when she decides to resume work, there is one person (a professional cellist‚ÄîSaramago clearly knows music) who somehow manages to elude her. So she visits his home, watches him walking his dog, attends his rehearsals and one of his performances, and finds herself falling in love. The book, which had begun as an intellectual game, ends in warm humanity. Medieval artists used to depict the Dance of Death as a sinister reaping by a skeleton with a scythe. Saramago gives us a dance <i>with</i> death, in the form of an attractive young woman who comes into our willing embrace. Would it were ever so!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15459863049790478171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1338456963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1338456963" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1338456963?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=21st-centurylit">21st-centurylit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=biddles">biddles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=society-went-boom">society-went-boom</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1338456963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12148688331419617180">At midnight on January 1, all death ceases. No one dies. People rejoice! Huzzah, no more death! People love this, because if there is one thing people love trying to accomplish in life, it is how to trick death.<br/><br/>But then, also typically, there is an "Oh, shit" moment. As in "Oh, shit; no one dies... that's actually not so great."<br/><br/>Think about no one dying. What do we do with the perpetually dying? All the old and infirm that are just... dying, no relief in sight? Who takes care of them, and for how</span>
  <span id="freeText12148688331419617180" style="display:none">At midnight on January 1, all death ceases. No one dies. People rejoice! Huzzah, no more death! People love this, because if there is one thing people love trying to accomplish in life, it is how to trick death.<br /><br />But then, also typically, there is an "Oh, shit" moment. As in "Oh, shit; no one dies... that's actually not so great."<br /><br />Think about no one dying. What do we do with the perpetually dying? All the old and infirm that are just... dying, no relief in sight? Who takes care of them, and for how long? Aging hasn't changed, everyone ages just at the same rate as they did before. They just don't die anymore. What about life insurance policies? Population? Think about it. It sounds awful.<br /><br />That's what Saramago investigates here, primarily in the first half of the book. From joy to panic, and then there is the letter from death. No, not Death - death. Lower case. Because that's how she rolls. And this letter shakes things up even more.<br /><br />It's a refreshingly different novel. It's not typical, in style (punctuation optional) to subject matter. And, equally refreshing, is the amount of humor Saramago used throughout. One would think a story about death (and the lack thereof) would be pretty morbid and grim, but this was actually entertaining and amusing.<br /><br />I dug it. Maybe not as much as I dug <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness__Blindness___1_" title="Blindness (Blindness, #1) by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Blindness</a>, but enough to want to read more of the Saramago I have on my shelves. He's one of those writers who, from my small experience, takes things in a completely different direction and looks at the world in a slightly different way from most participants. I like that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12148688331419617180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_252339347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/252339347" />
    <a title="Amy Bailey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3001459-amy-bailey"><img alt="Amy Bailey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588701842p2/3001459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/252339347?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252339347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1135312882250492526">First of all, it bothers me that writers who don't use appropriate punctuation and rules of correct writing are lauded as geniuses and rebels. Punctuation doesn't convey the message of the book. Punctuation makes sure I can understand what I read. Quotation marks aren't negotiable. That's the main thing that irked me about this book. <br/><br/>Yes, I like the premise. The personification of death was done in an almost humorous way. I have to say, however, I think I liked the second part of the book that r</span>
  <span id="freeText1135312882250492526" style="display:none">First of all, it bothers me that writers who don't use appropriate punctuation and rules of correct writing are lauded as geniuses and rebels. Punctuation doesn't convey the message of the book. Punctuation makes sure I can understand what I read. Quotation marks aren't negotiable. That's the main thing that irked me about this book. <br /><br />Yes, I like the premise. The personification of death was done in an almost humorous way. I have to say, however, I think I liked the second part of the book that revolved around the cellist better than I liked the first part. I found the writing a bit pretentious, which is only supported by the author's blatant disregard for grammatical etiquette. While I do respect what is being said about the importance of death in our world (i.e. - If you think the absence of death a good thing, you are severely deluded) and the insightful way the political, civic and economic ramifications of its absence were woven into the story, I just didn't find myself enjoying this very much. I have noticed the older I get, the less I care about how scholarly and inventive something is and I just want a good story. This wasn't one of those good stories that holds my attention. I finished it, but only because I refuse to give up reading anything. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1135312882250492526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_508181026" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/508181026" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/508181026?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=fiction-from-portugal">fiction-from-portugal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer508181026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17286533973556750777">I'm going to pass on a star rating here because I can't come up with something at the moment, but just for the record, this was a fine book. A little disjointed there as one part moves into the other which sort of threw me but overall I enjoyed it.<br/> **<br/><br/>There's an interview at <i>LA Weekly</i> with the author that I read after I'd finished this book some time back where he says the following:<br/><br/>"It‚Äôs not that I‚Äôm laughing at death, because no one can laugh at it. But why take it so seriously?"<br/><br/>Like the author</span>
  <span id="freeText17286533973556750777" style="display:none">I'm going to pass on a star rating here because I can't come up with something at the moment, but just for the record, this was a fine book. A little disjointed there as one part moves into the other which sort of threw me but overall I enjoyed it.<br /> **<br /><br />There's an interview at <i>LA Weekly</i> with the author that I read after I'd finished this book some time back where he says the following:<br /><br />"It‚Äôs not that I‚Äôm laughing at death, because no one can laugh at it. But why take it so seriously?"<br /><br />Like the author, we may not laugh at death, but in this novel there's a lot of black humor that surfaces surrounding death, or rather its absence, as life in one unnamed country changes when, on New Year's Day "no one died." <br /><br />"Up until the very dot of midnight on the last day of the year there were people who died in full compliance with the rules..."<br /><br />but everything changed since that "zero hour" and "there was no record in the whole country of anyone dying." At first, this phenomenon was seen by some as "humanity's greatest dream since the beginning of time," and after a few days, even the pessimists and skeptics joined others in the streets "to proclaim that life is beautiful." It doesn't take too long, though, for the problems inherent in such a situation to arise, and they do so in a very big way. And as we become involved in the crisis, suddenly something strange happens when death changes the rules between herself and the "mortals." <br /><br />Saramago takes us along during this crisis for the responses from politicians and the government, the Church, philosophers, the people themselves, the criminal underworld, and especially the media. There is more than a touch of irony here that makes for simultaneous fun and serious reading, and I kind of see it as a way in which he asks his readers to question the answers received from all of these institutions in the event of a crisis. I could be off base here, but certainly it's something I'll take away from this novel. <br /><br />I've seen some reader reviews of this novel that call it a "philosophical novel," and while the author is definitely asking people to think here, for me it goes well beyond that label. There's a lot going on in here with language and ideas, which along with the main story make this novel an experience and a pleasure to read. While some people have commented on their dislike of Saramago's writing style, I have to disagree. I think his style is actually rather brilliant. In fact, I enjoyed the book immensely, heard myself laughing through parts of it and I can certainly recommend it. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.readingavidly.com/2017/09/death-with-interruptions-by-jose.html">http://www.readingavidly.com/2017/09/...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17286533973556750777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6938582152143292171">A surreal modern parable full of imagination and quirky black humour. If, like me, you have read Saramago's earlier novel Blindness, this one has a similar structure. This one starts with the premise that in a small country, all death suddenly stops. The first half of the book explores the consequences of this, and the second half introduces the traditional personication of death, who realises that her work is not being monitored and starts to rebel. Saramago's style involves long convoluted sen</span>
  <span id="freeText6938582152143292171" style="display:none">A surreal modern parable full of imagination and quirky black humour. If, like me, you have read Saramago's earlier novel Blindness, this one has a similar structure. This one starts with the premise that in a small country, all death suddenly stops. The first half of the book explores the consequences of this, and the second half introduces the traditional personication of death, who realises that her work is not being monitored and starts to rebel. Saramago's style involves long convoluted sentences, but this is not a difficult book to read.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer853611751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5443583462601390521">On the first day of the year, no one dies. Death is taking a break, working on her tan and sipping cocktails in bikinis on the sunny coast of Algarve. Well, this is only my assumption and, obviously, a faulty one because death, who, as we all know, is a mere skeleton wrapped in a sheet, can't get a tan, not to mention the sight of her in a bikini would deal a massive blow to the tourism industry in the area. A more logical guess would be that she went to a more private place, like Mars. Easier t</span>
  <span id="freeText5443583462601390521" style="display:none">On the first day of the year, no one dies. Death is taking a break, working on her tan and sipping cocktails in bikinis on the sunny coast of Algarve. Well, this is only my assumption and, obviously, a faulty one because death, who, as we all know, is a mere skeleton wrapped in a sheet, can't get a tan, not to mention the sight of her in a bikini would deal a massive blow to the tourism industry in the area. A more logical guess would be that she went to a more private place, like Mars. Easier to resit the temptation of stepping back into the job, too. <br /><br />Don‚Äôt fret. There's still blood and suffering and human bodies injured beyond recognition and people getting old. Only now they're old forever and the pain never stops! And, as with any breach in normality, death's absence comes with terrible consequences: undertakers left with nothing to do and having to endure the humiliation of burying turtles and cockatoos, hospitals overcrowded with immortal patients in a state of permanent dying, retiring homes stuck with the same irritating people, the old threatening to outgrow in number the younger population, insurance companies in danger of losing their clients. Immortality isn't as great a thing as one might expect, life's still crappy, now more than ever before. <br /><br />And before you ask if, in this country where no one dies, anyone crosses the border to see what happens, yes, they do. <br /><br />It's all you need to know about the book. If this premise didn't convince you, I don't know what will.<br /><br />(bumped down my rating from 4 to 3 because I hated the ending. hated it!)<br /></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gx9eDsgYYrHp61uPW8wjK4DeJoCYl8YUGBsC6yimWcm5OTfcc0ar2U7A7+6sOSn0VoHBSFYaSHNDZURa5eWrUw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14991978635003011222">Jos√© Saramago is one of the most important international writers of the last hundred years. Born in Portugal in 1922, he was in his sixties when he came to prominence as a writer with the publication of Baltasar and Blimunda. A huge body of work followed, translated into more than forty languages, and in 1998 he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature. Saramago died in June 2010.</span>
